<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about smoking of tobacco and other drugs. <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6016" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG/220px-Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG/330px-Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG/440px-Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganja_Smoking_-_Gangasagar_Fair_Transit_Camp_-_Kolkata_2013-01-12_2646.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man smoking cannabis in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kolkata,_India" title="Kolkata, India">Kolkata, India</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smoking_Crack.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Smoking_Crack.jpg/220px-Smoking_Crack.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Smoking_Crack.jpg/330px-Smoking_Crack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Smoking_Crack.jpg/440px-Smoking_Crack.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smoking_Crack.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woman smoking crack cocaine</div></div></div>
<p><b>Smoking</b> is a practice in which a substance is burned and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a> is typically breathed in to be tasted and absorbed into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloodstream" title="Bloodstream">bloodstream</a>. Most commonly, the substance used is the dried leaves of the <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> plant, which have been rolled into a small rectangle of rolling paper to create a small, round cylinder called a <a href="/wiki/Cigarette" title="Cigarette">cigarette</a>. Smoking is primarily practised as a <a href="/wiki/Route_of_administration" title="Route of administration">route of administration</a> for <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">recreational drug use</a> because the <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of the dried plant leaves vaporizes and delivers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_substance" title="Active substance">active substances</a> into the lungs where they are rapidly absorbed into the bloodstream and reach bodily tissue. In the case of cigarette smoking, these substances are contained in a mixture of aerosol particles and gases and include the pharmacologically active alkaloid <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">nicotine</a>; the vaporization creates heated aerosol and gas into a form that allows inhalation and deep penetration into the lungs where absorption into the bloodstream of the active substances occurs. In some cultures, smoking is also carried out as a part of various rituals, where participants use it to help induce <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trance</a>-like states that, they believe, can lead them to <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(spiritual)" title="Enlightenment (spiritual)">spiritual enlightenment</a>.
</p><p>Smoking is one of the most common forms of recreational drug use. Tobacco smoking is the most popular form, being practised by over one billion people globally, of whom the majority are in the <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Less common drugs for smoking include <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_smoking" title="Cannabis smoking">cannabis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>. Some of the substances are classified as hard <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotics</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a>, but the use of these is very limited as they are usually not commercially available. Cigarettes are primarily industrially manufactured but also can be hand-rolled from loose tobacco and <a href="/wiki/Rolling_paper" title="Rolling paper">rolling paper</a>. Other smoking implements include <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_pipe" title="Tobacco pipe">pipes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cigar" title="Cigar">cigars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beedi" title="Beedi">bidis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hookah" title="Hookah">hookahs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bong" title="Bong">bongs</a>.
</p><p>Smoking has <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_tobacco" title="Health effects of tobacco">negative health effects</a>, because smoke inhalation inherently poses challenges to various <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiologic</a> processes such as <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiration</a>. Smoking tobacco is among the leading causes of many diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">COPD</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erectile_dysfunction" title="Erectile dysfunction">erectile dysfunction</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital_disorder" title="Congenital disorder">birth defects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Diseases related to <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">tobacco smoking</a> have been shown to kill approximately half of long-term smokers when compared to average mortality rates faced by non-smokers. Smoking caused over five million deaths a year from 1990 to 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet5April2017_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet5April2017-2">[2]</a></sup> Non-smokers account for 2% of all deaths globally due to <a href="/wiki/Passive_smoking" title="Passive smoking">second-hand smoke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">[3]</a></sup> The health hazards of smoking have caused many countries to institute high taxes on tobacco products, publish advertisements to discourage use, limit advertisements that promote use, and provide help with quitting for those who do smoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smoking can be dated to as early as 5000 BCE, and has been recorded in many different cultures across the world. Early smoking evolved in association with religious ceremonies; as offerings to deities; in cleansing rituals; or to allow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaman" title="Shaman">shamans</a> and priests to alter their minds for purposes of divination or spiritual enlightenment. After the European exploration and conquest of the Americas, the practice of smoking tobacco quickly spread to the rest of the world. In regions like India and Sub-Saharan Africa, it merged with existing practices of smoking (mostly of cannabis). In Europe, it introduced a new type of social activity and a form of drug intake which previously had been unknown.
</p><p>Perception surrounding smoking has varied over time and from one place to another: holy and sinful, sophisticated and vulgar, a <a href="/wiki/Panacea_(medicine)" title="Panacea (medicine)">panacea</a> and deadly health hazard. In the last decade of the 20th century, smoking came to be viewed in a decidedly negative light, especially in Western countries.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_smoking" title="History of smoking">History of smoking</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_uses">Early uses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aztec_feast_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="966" data-file-width="910" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Aztec_feast_1.jpg/220px-Aztec_feast_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Aztec_feast_1.jpg/330px-Aztec_feast_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Aztec_feast_1.jpg/440px-Aztec_feast_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aztec_feast_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aztec women are handed flowers and smoking tubes before eating at a banquet, <a href="/wiki/Florentine_Codex" title="Florentine Codex">Florentine Codex</a>, 1500</div></div></div>
<p>The history of smoking dates back to as early as 5000 BCE for shamanistic rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Many ancient civilizations, such as the Babylonians, Indians and Chinese, burnt incense as a part of religious rituals, as did the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> and the later Catholic and Orthodox Christian churches. Smoking in the Americas probably had its origins in the incense-burning ceremonies of <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamans</a> but was later adopted for pleasure, or as a social tool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The smoking of tobacco, as well as various <a href="/wiki/Hallucinogen" title="Hallucinogen">hallucinogenic</a> drugs, was used to achieve trances and to come into contact with the spirit world.
</p><p>Substances such as <a href="/wiki/Cannabis" title="Cannabis">cannabis</a>, clarified butter (<a href="/wiki/Ghee" title="Ghee">ghee</a>), fish offal, dried snake skins and various pastes molded around <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a> sticks dates back at least 2000 years. Fumigation (<i>dhupa</i>) and fire offerings (<i>homa</i>) are prescribed in the <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a> for medical purposes, and have been practiced for at least 3,000 years while smoking, <i>dhumrapana</i> (literally "drinking smoke"), has been practiced for at least 2,000 years. Before modern times these substances have been consumed through <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_pipe" title="Tobacco pipe">pipes</a>, with stems of various lengths or <a href="/wiki/Chillum_(pipe)" title="Chillum (pipe)">chillums</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Archaeological findings also show the existence of pipes to smoke <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> as soon as the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cannabis smoking was common in the Middle East before the arrival of tobacco, and was early on a common social activity that centered around the type of water pipe called a <a href="/wiki/Hookah" title="Hookah">hookah</a>. Smoking, especially after the introduction of tobacco, was an essential component of Muslim society and culture and became integrated with important traditions such as weddings, funerals and was expressed in architecture, clothing, literature and poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilmanXun200420–21_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilmanXun200420–21-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cannabis smoking was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> through <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopia</a> and the east African coast by either Indian or Arab traders in the 13th century or earlier and spread on the same trade routes as those that carried coffee, which originated in the highlands of Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> It was smoked in <a href="/wiki/Calabash" title="Calabash">calabash</a> water pipes with <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> smoking bowls, apparently an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People_of_Ethiopia" title="People of Ethiopia">Ethiopian</a> invention which was later conveyed to eastern, southern and central Africa.
</p><p>Reports from the first European explorers and conquistadors to reach the Americas tell of rituals where native priests smoked themselves into such high degrees of intoxication that it is unlikely that the rituals were limited to just tobacco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularization">Popularization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more about the impact and development of tobacco, see <a href="/wiki/History_of_tobacco" title="History of tobacco">History of tobacco</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more about the commercial development of tobacco, see <a href="/wiki/History_of_commercial_tobacco_in_the_United_States" title="History of commercial tobacco in the United States">History of commercial tobacco in the United States</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persian_girl_smoking.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1303" data-file-width="888" decoding="async" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Persian_girl_smoking.jpg/220px-Persian_girl_smoking.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Persian_girl_smoking.jpg/330px-Persian_girl_smoking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Persian_girl_smoking.jpg/440px-Persian_girl_smoking.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persian_girl_smoking.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A Persian girl smoking</i> by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_Qasim_(painter)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Muhammad Qasim (painter) (page does not exist)">Muhammad Qasim</a>, 17th century.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1612, six years after the settlement of <a href="/wiki/Jamestown,_Virginia" title="Jamestown, Virginia">Jamestown</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Rolfe" title="John Rolfe">John Rolfe</a> was credited as the first settler to successfully grow tobacco as a <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crop</a>. The demand quickly grew as tobacco, referred to as "golden weed", revived the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Company" title="Virginia Company">Virginia Company</a> from its failed expeditions in search for gold in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In order to meet demands from the old world, tobacco was grown in succession, quickly depleting the land. This became a motivator to settle west into the unknown continent, and likewise an expansion of tobacco production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indentured_servitude" title="Indentured servitude">Indentured servants</a> became the primary labor force up until <a href="/wiki/Bacon%27s_Rebellion" title="Bacon's Rebellion">Bacon's Rebellion</a>, from which the focus turned to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_colonial_United_States" title="Slavery in the colonial United States">slavery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> This trend abated following the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> as slavery became regarded as unprofitable. However the practice was revived in 1794 with the invention of the cotton gin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Frenchman named <a href="/wiki/Jean_Nicot" title="Jean Nicot">Jean Nicot</a> (from whose name the word nicotine is derived) introduced tobacco to <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> in 1560. From France tobacco spread to England. The first report documents an English sailor in Bristol in 1556, seen "emitting smoke from his nostrils".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L&amp;M_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-L&amp;M-15">[15]</a></sup> Like tea, coffee and opium, tobacco was just one of many intoxicants that was originally used as a form of medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Tobacco was introduced around 1600 by French merchants in what today is modern-day <a href="/wiki/The_Gambia" title="The Gambia">The Gambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>. At the same time caravans from <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> brought tobacco to the areas around <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a> and the Portuguese brought the commodity (and the plant) to southern Africa, establishing the popularity of tobacco throughout all of Africa by the 1650s.
</p><p>Soon after its introduction to the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, tobacco came under frequent criticism from state and religious leaders. <a href="/wiki/Murad_IV" title="Murad IV">Murad IV</a>, sultan of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> 1623–40 was among the first to attempt a smoking ban by claiming it was a threat to public morality and health. The <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a> of China issued an edict banning smoking two years before his death and the overthrow of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>. Later, the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a> rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, would proclaim smoking "a more heinous crime than that even of neglecting archery". In <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> Japan, some of the earliest tobacco plantations were scorned by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dgun" title="Shōgun">shōgun</a></i> as being a threat to the military economy by letting valuable farmland go to waste for the use of a recreational drug instead of being used to plant food crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Screech-Smoke_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Screech-Smoke-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bonsack_machine.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1584" data-file-width="1897" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bonsack_machine.png/220px-Bonsack_machine.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bonsack_machine.png/330px-Bonsack_machine.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bonsack_machine.png/440px-Bonsack_machine.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bonsack_machine.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonsack's cigarette rolling machine, as shown on U.S. patent 238,640</div></div></div>
<p>Religious leaders have often been prominent among those who considered smoking immoral or outright blasphemous. In 1634, the <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Moscow_and_all_Rus%27" title="Patriarch of Moscow and all Rus'">Patriarch of Moscow and all Rus'</a> forbade the sale of tobacco and sentenced men and women who flouted the ban to have their nostrils slit and their backs whipped until skin came off their backs. The Western church leader <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_VII" title="Pope Urban VII">Pope Urban VII</a> likewise condemned smoking in a papal bull of 1590. Despite many concerted efforts, restrictions and bans were almost universally ignored. When <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James VI and I</a>, a staunch anti-smoker and the author of <i><a href="/wiki/A_Counterblaste_to_Tobacco" title="A Counterblaste to Tobacco">A Counterblaste to Tobacco</a></i>, tried to curb the new trend by enforcing a whopping 4000% tax increase on tobacco in 1604, it proved a failure, as London had some 7,000 tobacco sellers by the early 17th century. Later, scrupulous rulers would realise the futility of smoking bans and instead turned tobacco trade and cultivation into lucrative government monopolies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilmanXun200415–16_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilmanXun200415–16-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-17th century every major civilization had been introduced to tobacco smoking and in many cases had already assimilated it into its culture, despite the attempts of many rulers to stamp the practice out with harsh penalties or fines. Tobacco, both product, and plant followed the major trade routes to major ports and markets, and then on into the hinterlands. The English language term <i>smoking</i> was coined in the late 18th century; before then the practice was referred to as <i>drinking smoke</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L&amp;M_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-L&amp;M-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tobacco and cannabis were used in Sub-Saharan Africa, much like elsewhere in the world, to confirm social relations, but also created entirely new ones. In what is today <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Congo</a>, a society called Bena Diemba ("People of Cannabis") was organized in the late 19th century in Lubuko ("The Land of Friendship"). The Bena Diemba were collectivist pacifists that rejected alcohol and herbal medicines in favor of cannabis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts200453–54_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts200453–54-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The growth remained stable until the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> in the 1860s, from which the primary labor force transition from slavery to <a href="/wiki/Sharecropping" title="Sharecropping">sharecropping</a>. This compounded with a change in demand, lead to the industrialization of tobacco production with the cigarette. <a href="/wiki/James_Albert_Bonsack" title="James Albert Bonsack">James Albert Bonsack</a>, a craftsman, in 1881 produced a machine to speed the production of cigarettes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_134–135_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_134–135-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opium">Opium</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">Opium</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:French_opium_den.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1729" data-file-width="1379" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/French_opium_den.jpg/170px-French_opium_den.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/French_opium_den.jpg/255px-French_opium_den.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/French_opium_den.jpg/340px-French_opium_den.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:French_opium_den.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of an <a href="/wiki/Opium_den" title="Opium den">opium den</a> on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Petit_Journal_(newspaper)" title="Le Petit Journal (newspaper)">Le Petit Journal</a></i>, 5 July 1903</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, the practise of smoking opium became widespread in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">China</a>. Previously, opium had only been ingested via consumption, and then only for its medicinal properties (opium was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthetic" title="Anaesthetic">anaesthetic</a>). The narcotic was also outlawed in China sometime in the early 18th century due the societal issues it caused. Due to a massive trade imbalance, however, foreign merchants started to smuggle opium into China via <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Canton</a>, to the chagrin of the Chinese authorities. Attempts by Chinese official <a href="/wiki/Lin_Zexu" title="Lin Zexu">Lin Zexu</a> to eliminate the trade led to the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a>. The Chinese defeat in the First and <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium Wars</a> resulted in the legalization of the importation of opium into China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Josh_ten_Berge_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Josh_ten_Berge-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Opium smoking later spread with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_diaspora" title="Chinese diaspora">Chinese immigrants</a> and spawned many infamous <a href="/wiki/Opium_den" title="Opium den">opium dens</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chinatown" title="Chinatown">Chinatowns</a> around South and Southeast Asia, Europe and the Americas. In the latter half of the 19th century, opium smoking became popular in the artistic community in Europe, especially Paris; artists' neighborhoods such as <a href="/wiki/Montparnasse" title="Montparnasse">Montparnasse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montmartre" title="Montmartre">Montmartre</a> became virtual "opium capitals". While opium dens that catered primarily to emigrant Chinese continued to exist in Chinatowns around the world, the trend among the European artists largely abated after the outbreak of World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Josh_ten_Berge_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Josh_ten_Berge-21">[21]</a></sup> The consumption of Opium abated in China during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> in the 1960s and 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Josh_ten_Berge_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Josh_ten_Berge-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-tobacco_movement">Anti-tobacco movement</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more about the movement in the 1930s and 1940s, see <a href="/wiki/Anti-tobacco_movement_in_Nazi_Germany" title="Anti-tobacco movement in Nazi Germany">Anti-tobacco movement in Nazi Germany</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more about the modern movement, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-smoking_movement" title="Anti-smoking movement">Anti-smoking movement</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more about the development of public policy, see <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_politics" title="Tobacco politics">Tobacco politics</a>.</div>
<p>Many people have been critical about tobacco use since it gained popularity. In 1798, Dr. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Rush" title="Benjamin Rush">Benjamin Rush</a> (early American physician, signer of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgeon_General" title="Surgeon General">Surgeon General</a> under <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, and anti-tobacco activist) was "against the habitual use of tobacco" because he believed it (a) "led to a desire for strong drink," (b) "was injurious both to health and morals," (c) "is generally offensive to" nonsmokers, (d) "produces a want of respect for" nonsmokers, and (e) "always disposes to unkind and unjust behavior towards them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the modernization of cigarette production compounded with the increased life expectancies during the 1920s, adverse health effects began to become more prevalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-1">[1]</a></sup> In Germany, anti-smoking groups, often associated with anti-liquor groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWC178_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWC178-25">[25]</a></sup> first published advocacy against the consumption of tobacco in the journal <i>Der Tabakgegner</i> (The Tobacco Opponent) in 1912 and 1932. In 1929, <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Lickint" title="Fritz Lickint">Fritz Lickint</a> of Dresden, Germany, published a paper containing formal statistical evidence of a lung cancer–tobacco link. During the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> condemned his earlier smoking habit as a waste of money,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWC219_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWC219-26">[26]</a></sup> and later with stronger assertions. This movement was further strengthened with Nazi reproductive policy as women who smoked were viewed as unsuitable to be wives and mothers in a German family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWC187_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWC187-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The movement in Nazi Germany did reach across enemy lines during the Second World War, as anti-smoking groups quickly lost popular support.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By the end of the Second World War, American cigarette manufacturers quickly reentered the German black market. Illegal smuggling of tobacco became prevalent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWC245_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWC245-28">[28]</a></sup> and leaders of the Nazi anti-smoking campaign were assassinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADLNMPHP_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADLNMPHP-29">[29]</a></sup> As part of the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, the United States shipped free tobacco to Germany; with 24,000 tons in 1948 and 69,000 tons in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWC245_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-NWC245-28">[28]</a></sup> Per capita yearly cigarette consumption in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Germany_(1945%E2%80%9390)" title="History of Germany (1945–90)">post-war Germany</a> steadily rose from 460 in 1950 to 1,523 in 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWC228_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWC228-30">[30]</a></sup> By the end of the 20th century, anti-smoking campaigns in Germany were unable to exceed the effectiveness of the Nazi-era climax in the years 1939–41 and German tobacco health research was described by <a href="/wiki/Robert_N._Proctor" title="Robert N. Proctor">Robert N. Proctor</a> as "muted".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWC228_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-NWC228-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="253" data-file-width="514" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg/220px-Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg/330px-Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg/440px-Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lung_Cancer_Incidece_vers_Cigarette_Consumption.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A lengthy study conducted in order to establish the strong association necessary for legislative action (US cigarette consumption per person blue, male lung cancer rate green)</div></div></div>
<p>In the UK and the US, an increase in lung cancer rates, formerly "among the rarest forms of disease", was noted by the 1930s, but its cause remained unknown and even the credibility of this increase was sometimes disputed as late as 1950. For example, in Connecticut, reported age-adjusted incidence rates of lung cancer among males increased 220% between 1935–39 and 1950–54. In the UK, the share of lung cancer among all cancer deaths in men increased from 1.5% in 1920 to 19.7% in 1947. Nevertheless, these increases were questioned as potentially caused by increased reporting and improved methods of diagnosis. Although several carcinogens were already known at the time (for example, <a href="/wiki/Benzo(a)pyrene" title="Benzo(a)pyrene">benzo[a]pyrene</a> was isolated from coal tar and demonstrated to be a potent carcinogen in 1933), none were known to be contained in adequate quantities in <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoke" title="Tobacco smoke">tobacco smoke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Doll" title="Richard Doll">Richard Doll</a> in 1950 published research in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" title="British Medical Journal">British Medical Journal</a> showing a close link between smoking and lung cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Four years later, in 1954 the <a href="/wiki/British_Doctors_Study" title="British Doctors Study">British Doctors Study</a>, a study of some 40 thousand doctors over 20 years, confirmed the link, based on which the government issued advice that smoking and lung cancer rates were related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichardHillyBMJ1954_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-RichardHillyBMJ1954-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1964 the United States <a href="/wiki/Surgeon_General_of_the_United_States" title="Surgeon General of the United States">Surgeon General</a>'s Report on Smoking and Health demonstrated the relationship between smoking and cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Further reports confirmed this link in the 1980s and concluded in 1986 that <a href="/wiki/Passive_smoking" title="Passive smoking">passive smoking</a> was also harmful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>As scientific evidence mounted in the 1980s, tobacco companies claimed <a href="/wiki/Contributory_negligence" title="Contributory negligence">contributory negligence</a> as the adverse health effects were previously unknown or lacked substantial credibility. Health authorities sided with these claims up until 1998, from which they reversed their position. The <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_Master_Settlement_Agreement" title="Tobacco Master Settlement Agreement">Tobacco Master Settlement Agreement</a>, originally between the four largest US tobacco companies and the Attorneys General of 46 states, restricted certain types of tobacco advertisement and required payments for health compensation; which later amounted to the largest civil settlement in United States history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WallStreetJournalTobaccoMastersSettlement_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-WallStreetJournalTobaccoMastersSettlement-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1965 to 2006, rates of smoking in the United States have declined from 42% to 20.8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RockEtAlCDC2006_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-RockEtAlCDC2006-38">[38]</a></sup> A significant majority of those who quit were professional, affluent men. Despite this decrease in the prevalence of consumption, the average number of cigarettes consumed per person per day increased from 22 in 1954 to 30 in 1978. This paradoxical event suggests that those who quit smoked less, while those who continued to smoke moved to smoke more light cigarettes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This trend has been paralleled by many industrialized nations as rates have either leveled-off or declined. In the <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>, however, tobacco consumption continues to rise at 3.4% in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2002FactSheet_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2002FactSheet-40">[40]</a></sup> In Africa, smoking is in most areas considered to be modern, and many of the strong adverse opinions that prevail in the West receive much less attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts200446–57_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts200446–57-41">[41]</a></sup> Today <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> leads as the top consumer of tobacco followed by <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the global scale, initial ideas of an international convention towards the prevention of tobacco had been initiated in the World Health Assembly (WHA) in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1998, along with the successful election of <a href="/wiki/Gro_Harlem_Brundtland" title="Gro Harlem Brundtland">Dr. Gro Harlem Brundtland</a> as the Director-General, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> set tobacco control as its leading health concern and has begun a program known as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tobacco_Free_Initiative&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tobacco Free Initiative (page does not exist)">Tobacco Free Initiative</a> (TFI) in order to reduce rates of consumption in the developing world. However, it was not until 2003 that the Framework Convention on Tobacco Control (FCTC) was accepted in WHA and entered into force in 2005. FCTC marked a milestone as the first international treaty concerning a global health issue that aims to combat tobacco in multiple aspects including tobacco taxes, advertisement, trading, environmental affects, health influences, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The birth of this evidence-based and systematic approach has resulted in the reinforcement of tobacco taxes and the implementation of smoke-free laws in 128 countries that led to the decrease of smoking prevalence in developing nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_substances">Other substances</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more about the rise of crack cocaine, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crack_epidemic" title="Crack epidemic">Crack epidemic</a>.</div>
<p>In the early 1980s, organized international trafficking of <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> grew. However, overproduction and tighter legal enforcement for the illegal product caused drug dealers to convert the powder to "crack" – a solid, smokable form of cocaine that could be sold in smaller quantities to more people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> This trend abated in the 1990s as increased police action coupled with a robust economy caused many potential consumers to give up or fail to take up the habit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent years shows an increase in the consumption of vaporized heroin, <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">methamphetamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phencyclidine" title="Phencyclidine">Phencyclidine</a> (PCP). Along with a smaller number of <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_drug" title="Psychedelic drug">psychedelic drugs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Changa_(drug)" title="Changa (drug)">Changa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimethyltryptamine" title="Dimethyltryptamine">DMT</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-Meo-DMT" title="5-Meo-DMT">5-Meo-DMT</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salvia_divinorum" title="Salvia divinorum">Salvia divinorum</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Substances_and_equipment">Substances and equipment</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use#List_of_drugs_which_can_be_smoked" title="Recreational drug use">List of drugs which can be smoked</a></div>
<p>The most popular type of substance that is smoked is <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">tobacco</a>. There are many different tobacco cultivars which are made into a wide variety of mixtures and brands. Tobacco is often sold flavored, often with various fruit aromas, something which is especially popular for use with water pipes, such as <a href="/wiki/Hookah" title="Hookah">hookahs</a>. The second most common substance that is smoked is <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_smoking" title="Cannabis smoking">cannabis</a>, made from the flowers or leaves of <i><a href="/wiki/Cannabis_sativa" title="Cannabis sativa">Cannabis sativa</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Cannabis_indica" title="Cannabis indica">Cannabis indica</a></i>. The substance is considered illegal in most countries in the world and in the countries that tolerate public consumption, it is sometimes only pseudo-legal. Despite this, a considerable percentage of the adult population in many countries have tried it with smaller minorities doing it on a regular basis. Since cannabis is illegal or only tolerated in many jurisdictions, there is no industrial mass-production of cigarettes, meaning that the most common form of smoking is with hand-rolled cigarettes (often called <a href="/wiki/Joint_(cannabis)" title="Joint (cannabis)">joints</a>) or with pipes. Water pipes are also fairly common; water pipes used for cannabis include designs known as bongs and bubblers, among others.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg/200px-Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg/300px-Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg/400px-Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fangspipe_ubt.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An elaborately decorated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoking_pipe_(tobacco)" title="Smoking pipe (tobacco)">pipe</a></div></div></div>
<p>A few other recreational drugs are smoked by smaller minorities. Most of these substances are <a href="/wiki/Controlled_substance" title="Controlled substance">controlled</a>, and some are considerably more intoxicating than either tobacco or cannabis. These include <a href="/wiki/Crack_cocaine" title="Crack cocaine">crack cocaine</a>, heroin, <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">methamphetamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phencyclidine" title="Phencyclidine">PCP</a>. A small number of <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_drug" title="Psychedelic drug">psychedelic drugs</a> are also smoked, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimethyltryptamine" title="Dimethyltryptamine">DMT</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-Meo-DMT" title="5-Meo-DMT">5-Meo-DMT</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salvia_divinorum" title="Salvia divinorum">Salvia divinorum</a>.
</p><p>Even the most primitive form of smoking requires tools of some sort to perform. This has resulted in a staggering variety of smoking tools and paraphernalia from all over the world. Whether tobacco, cannabis, opium or herbs, some form of receptacle is required along with a source of fire to light the mixture. The most common today is by far the cigarette, consisting of a mild inhalant strain of tobacco in a tightly rolled tube of paper, usually manufactured industrially and including a <a href="/wiki/Cigarette_filter" title="Cigarette filter">filter</a>, or hand-rolled with loose tobacco. Other popular smoking tools are various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoking_pipe_(tobacco)" title="Smoking pipe (tobacco)">pipes</a> and cigars.
</p><p>A less common but increasingly popular alternative to smoking is vaporizers, which use hot air convection to deliver the substance without combustion, which may reduce health risks. A portable vaporization alternative appeared in 2003 with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Electronic_cigarette" title="Electronic cigarette">electronic cigarettes</a>, battery-operated, cigarette-shaped devices which produce an aerosol intended to mimic the smoke from burning tobacco, delivering nicotine to the user without some of the harmful substances released in tobacco smoke.
</p><p>Other than actual smoking equipment, many other items are associated with smoking; <a href="/wiki/Cigarette_case" title="Cigarette case">cigarette cases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cigar_box" title="Cigar box">cigar boxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lighter" title="Lighter">lighters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Match" title="Match">matchboxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cigarette_holder" title="Cigarette holder">cigarette holders</a>, cigar holders, <a href="/wiki/Ashtray" title="Ashtray">ashtrays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silent_butler" title="Silent butler">silent butlers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pipe_cleaner" title="Pipe cleaner">pipe cleaners</a>, tobacco cutters, match stands, pipe tampers, cigarette companions and so on. Some examples of these have become valuable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collector_item" title="Collector item">collector items</a> and particularly ornate and antique items can fetch high prices.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3104" data-file-width="3858" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png/350px-Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png/525px-Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png/700px-Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Risks_form_smoking-smoking_can_damage_every_part_of_the_body.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram of the human body showing some of the diseases caused by smoking</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_tobacco" title="Health effects of tobacco">Health effects of tobacco</a></div>
<p>Smoking is one of the leading causes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_preventable_causes_of_death" title="List of preventable causes of death">preventable death</a> globally and is the cause of 15% of all deaths, 2% of which are non-smokers who die due to <a href="/wiki/Passive_smoking" title="Passive smoking">second-hand smoke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-3">[3]</a></sup> In the United States, about 500,000 deaths per year are attributed to smoking-related diseases and a recent study estimated that as much as 1/3 of China's male population will have significantly shortened life-spans due to smoking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Male and female smokers lose an average of 13.2 and 14.5 years of life, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12002168_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12002168-49">[49]</a></sup> At least half of all lifelong smokers die earlier as a result of smoking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15213107_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15213107-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7661229_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7661229-51">[51]</a></sup> The risk of dying from lung cancer before age 85 is 22.1% for a male smoker and 11.9% for a female current smoker, in the absence of competing causes of death. The corresponding estimates for lifelong nonsmokers are a 1.1% probability of dying from lung cancer before age 85 for a man of European descent, and a 0.8% probability for a woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Smoking just one cigarette a day results in a risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_heart_disease" title="Coronary heart disease">coronary heart disease</a> that is halfway between that of a heavy smoker and a non-smoker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-linear" title="Non-linear">non-linear</a> <a href="/wiki/Dose%E2%80%93response_relationship" title="Dose–response relationship">dose–response relationship</a> may be explained by smoking's effect on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platelet_aggregation" title="Platelet aggregation">platelet aggregation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9365294_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9365294-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the diseases that can be caused by smoking are vascular <a href="/wiki/Stenosis" title="Stenosis">stenosis</a>, lung cancer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_attacks" title="Heart attacks">heart attacks</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nyboe-1989_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nyboe-1989-57">[57]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dev_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-dev-58">[58]</a></sup> Smoking during pregnancy may cause ADHD to a fetus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17185283_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17185283-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smoking is a risk factor strongly associated with <a href="/wiki/Periodontal_disease" title="Periodontal disease">periodontitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tooth_loss" title="Tooth loss">tooth loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The effects of smoking on <a href="/wiki/Periodontium" title="Periodontium">periodontal tissues</a> depend on the number of cigarettes smoked daily and the duration of the habit. A study showed that smokers had 2.7 times and former smokers 2.3 times greater probabilities to have established periodontal disease than non‐smokers, independent of age, sex and plaque index,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramon_771–776_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramon_771–776-61">[61]</a></sup> however, the effect of tobacco on periodontal tissues seems to be more pronounced in men than in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramon_771–776_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ramon_771–776-61">[61]</a></sup> Studies have found that smokers had greater odds for more severe dental bone loss compared to non‐smokers <a class="external autonumber" href="https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/abs/10.1902/jop.1995.66.1.23" rel="nofollow">[1]</a>, also, people who smoke and drink <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_drink" title="Alcoholic drink">alcohol</a> heavily have much higher <abbr>risk</abbr> of developing <a href="/wiki/Oral_cancer" title="Oral cancer"><abbr>oral</abbr> cancer</a> (mouth and lip) compared with people who do neither.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Smoking can also cause <a href="/wiki/Smoker%27s_melanosis" title="Smoker's melanosis">milanosis</a> in the mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smoking has been also associated with oral conditions including <a href="/wiki/Tooth_decay" title="Tooth decay">dental caries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dental_implant" title="Dental implant">dental implant failures</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premalignant_lesions" title="Premalignant lesions">premalignant lesions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oral_cancer" title="Oral cancer">cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Smoking can affect the immune-inflammatory processes which may increase the susceptibility to infections; it can alter the oral mycobiota and facilitate colonization of the oral cavity with fungi and pathogenic molds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many governments are trying to deter people from smoking with anti-smoking campaigns in mass media stressing the harmful long-term effects of smoking. <a href="/wiki/Passive_smoking" title="Passive smoking">Passive smoking</a>, or secondhand smoking, which affects people in the immediate vicinity of smokers, is a major reason for the enforcement of <a href="/wiki/Smoking_ban" title="Smoking ban">smoking bans</a>. These are laws enforced to stop individuals from smoking in indoor public places, such as bars, pubs and restaurants, thus reducing nonsmokers' exposure to secondhand smoke. A common concern among legislators is to discourage smoking among minors and many states have passed laws against selling tobacco products to underage customers (establishing a <a href="/wiki/Smoking_age" title="Smoking age">smoking age</a>). Many developing countries have not adopted anti-smoking policies, leading some to call for anti-smoking campaigns and further education to explain the negative effects of ETS (Environmental Tobacco Smoke) in developing countries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tobacco_advertising" title="Tobacco advertising">Tobacco advertising</a> is also sometimes regulated to make smoking less appealing.
</p><p>Despite the many bans, European countries still hold 18 of the top 20 spots, and according to the ERC, a market research company, the heaviest smokers are from Greece, averaging 3,000 cigarettes per person in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Rates of smoking have leveled off or declined in the developed world but continue to rise in developing countries. Smoking rates in the United States have dropped by half from 1965 to 2006, falling from 42% to 20.8% in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The effects of addiction on society vary considerably between different substances that can be smoked and the indirect social problems that they cause, in great part because of the differences in legislation and the enforcement of narcotics legislation around the world. Though nicotine is a highly addictive drug, its effects on cognition are not as intense or noticeable as other drugs such as cocaine, amphetamines or any of the <a href="/wiki/Opiate" title="Opiate">opiates</a> (including heroin and <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Smoking is a risk factor in <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> While smoking more than 15 cigarettes per day has been shown to worsen the symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn's disease">Crohn's disease</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> smoking has been shown to actually lower the prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Ulcerative_colitis" title="Ulcerative colitis">ulcerative colitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smokers are 30-40% more likely to develop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">type 2 diabetes</a> than non-smokers, and the risk increases with the number of cigarettes smoked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blood_nicotine_graph.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="513" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Blood_nicotine_graph.jpg/350px-Blood_nicotine_graph.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Blood_nicotine_graph.jpg/525px-Blood_nicotine_graph.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Blood_nicotine_graph.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blood_nicotine_graph.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A graph that shows the efficiency of smoking as a way to absorb nicotine compared to other forms of intake</div></div></div>
<p>Inhaling the vaporized gas form of substances into the lungs is a quick and very effective way of delivering drugs into the bloodstream (as the gas diffuses directly into the pulmonary vein, then into the heart and from there to the brain) and affects the user within less than a second of the first inhalation. The lungs consist of several million tiny bulbs called <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a> that altogether have an area of over 70 m<sup>2</sup> (about the area of a tennis court). This can be used to administer useful medical as well as recreational drugs such as aerosols, consisting of tiny droplets of a medication, or as gas produced by burning plant material with a psychoactive substance or pure forms of the substance itself. Not all drugs can be smoked, for example the sulphate derivative that is most commonly inhaled through the nose, though purer free base forms of substances can, but often require considerable skill in administering the drug properly. The method is also somewhat inefficient since not all of the smoke will be inhaled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The inhaled substances trigger chemical reactions in nerve endings in the brain due to being similar to naturally occurring substances such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endorphin" title="Endorphin">endorphins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a>, which are associated with sensations of pleasure. The result is what is usually referred to as a "high" that ranges between the mild stimulus caused by <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">nicotine</a> to the intense euphoria caused by heroin, <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methamphetamine" title="Methamphetamine">methamphetamines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Inhaling smoke into the lungs, no matter the substance, has adverse effects on one's health.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The incomplete combustion produced by burning plant material, like tobacco or cannabis, produces <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, which impairs the ability of blood to carry oxygen when inhaled into the lungs. There are several other toxic compounds in tobacco that constitute serious health hazards to long-term smokers from a whole range of causes; vascular abnormalities such as <a href="/wiki/Stenosis" title="Stenosis">stenosis</a>, lung cancer, heart attacks, strokes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impotence" title="Impotence">impotence</a>, low birth weight of infants born by <a href="/wiki/Smoking_and_pregnancy" title="Smoking and pregnancy">smoking mothers</a>. 8% of long-term smokers develop the characteristic set of facial changes known to doctors as <a href="/wiki/Smoker%27s_face" title="Smoker's face">smoker's face</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tobacco smoke is a complex mixture of over 5,000 identified chemicals, of which 98 are known to have specific <a href="/wiki/Toxicology" title="Toxicology">toxicological</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21556207_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21556207-79">[79]</a></sup> The most important chemicals <a href="/wiki/Carcinogenesis" title="Carcinogenesis">causing cancer</a> are those that produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_damage" title="DNA damage">DNA damage</a> since such damage appears to be the primary underlying cause of cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18403632_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18403632-80">[80]</a></sup> Cunningham et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cunningham_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham-81">[81]</a></sup> combined the microgram weight of the compound in the smoke of one cigarette with the known <a href="/wiki/Genotoxicity" title="Genotoxicity">genotoxic</a> effect per microgram to identify the most <a href="/wiki/Carcinogenesis" title="Carcinogenesis">carcinogenic</a> compounds in cigarette smoke. The seven most important carcinogens in tobacco smoke are shown in the table, along with DNA alterations they cause.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sigmund_Freud,_by_Max_Halberstadt_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2240" data-file-width="1647" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sigmund_Freud%2C_by_Max_Halberstadt_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Sigmund_Freud%2C_by_Max_Halberstadt_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sigmund_Freud%2C_by_Max_Halberstadt_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Sigmund_Freud%2C_by_Max_Halberstadt_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Sigmund_Freud%2C_by_Max_Halberstadt_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Sigmund_Freud%2C_by_Max_Halberstadt_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sigmund_Freud,_by_Max_Halberstadt_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>, whose doctor assisted with his suicide because of <a href="/wiki/Oral_cancer" title="Oral cancer">oral cancer</a> caused by smoking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gay_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gay-89">[89]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Most tobacco smokers begin during adolescence or early adulthood. Smoking has elements of risk-taking and rebellion, which often appeal to young people. The presence of high-status models and peers may also encourage smoking. Because teenagers are influenced more by their peers than by adults,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> attempts by parents, schools, and health professionals at preventing people from trying cigarettes are not always successful.
</p><p>Smokers often report that cigarettes help relieve feelings of stress. However, the stress levels of adult smokers are slightly higher than those of nonsmokers. Adolescent smokers report increasing levels of stress as they develop regular patterns of smoking, and <a href="/wiki/Smoking_cessation" title="Smoking cessation">smoking cessation</a> leads to reduced stress. Far from acting as an aid for mood control, nicotine dependency seems to exacerbate stress. This is confirmed in the daily mood patterns described by smokers, with normal moods during smoking and worsening moods between cigarettes. Thus, the apparent relaxant effect of smoking only reflects the reversal of the tension and irritability that develop during nicotine depletion. Dependent smokers need nicotine to remain feeling normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-20th century psychologists such as <a href="/wiki/Hans_Eysenck" title="Hans Eysenck">Hans Eysenck</a> developed a personality profile for the typical smoker of that period; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extraversion" title="Extraversion">extraversion</a> was associated with smoking, and smokers tended to be sociable, impulsive, risk taking, and excitement-seeking individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Although personality and social factors may make people likely to smoke, the actual habit is a function of <a href="/wiki/Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning">operant conditioning</a>. During the early stages, smoking provides pleasurable sensations (because of its action on the <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> system) and thus serves as a source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_reinforcement" title="Positive reinforcement">positive reinforcement</a>. After an individual has smoked for many years, the avoidance of withdrawal symptoms and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_reinforcement" title="Negative reinforcement">negative reinforcement</a> become the key motivations. Like all addictive substances, the amount of exposure required to become dependent on nicotine can vary from person to person.
</p><p>In terms of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Five_personality_traits" title="Big Five personality traits">Big Five personality traits</a>, research has found smoking to be correlated with lower levels of <a href="/wiki/Agreeableness" title="Agreeableness">agreeableness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conscientiousness" title="Conscientiousness">conscientiousness</a>, as well as higher levels of <a href="/wiki/Extraversion_and_introversion" title="Extraversion and introversion">extraversion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroticism" title="Neuroticism">neuroticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<p>Education and counselling by physicians of children and adolescents has been found to be effective in decreasing the risk of tobacco use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23974179_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23974179-94">[94]</a></sup> Systematic reviews show that psychosocial interventions can help women stop smoking in late pregnancy, reducing low birthweight and preterm births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> A 2016 Cochrane review showed that the combination of medication and behavioural support was more effective than minimal interventions or usual care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Another Cochrane review "suggests that neither reducing smoking to quit nor quitting abruptly results in superior quit rates; people could therefore be given a choice of how to quit, and support provided to people who would specifically like to reduce their smoking before quitting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 335px"><div style="width: 335px">
